0066620996|5.0|Of all the possible ways that you can make money in your business, only a few will make you truly great. At least that's the theory that Jim Collins talks about in his excellent book Good To Great. My favourite part is his hedgehog concept (stop reading here if you want to read this for yourself!).<br /><br />What he talks about in the book is that those companies that make it to great (and not many get this accolade from him!) do so by having a `hedgehog' concept. The `hedgehog' concept comes from the parable of the Fox and the Hedgehog. Foxes know lots of small things; they rush around with new ideas trying to find ways to eat the hedgehog. Hedgehogs know one big thing and know it well - and that is how to roll up into a thorny ball to fool the fox. Jim Collins teaches us all great companies and great thinkers do one thing, they do it deliberately and with deep insight and understanding, they do it consistently and with discipline and it makes them very successful.<br /><br />I love that he breaks this down for us into three components that any individual or business can apply:<br />Passions - what we love to do; Natural Abilities - he calls it genetic encoding - what we can be the best in the world at; and the Economic Driver - where the company can make money (or where we can add value as an individual).<br /><br />This is a really accessible, well-written, original work. It's one of those books that I come backk to again and again when I need a bit of insipration - can't wait to get on to his next one!
0385528752|4.0|Switch is not your typical change management tome.  There's no 10-Step Plan or Come-to-Consensus moment.  Instead, the Heath brothers present a clear 3-part framework based on scientific research of how the human brain works.  In short, the framework advocates appealing to individual's rational (Rider) and emotional (Elephant) sides, and shaping the change path.<br /><br />The book is broken into three sections - Direct the Rider, Motivate the Elephant and Shape the Path.  Consistent with their earlier book, Made to Stick, and their Fast Company column, Switch is replete with real-world anecdotes about identifying, motivating and executing difficult changes.<br /><br />One story that stuck out for me was Jerry Sternin's work in 1990 with Save the Children in Vietnam.  Save the Children was invited to Vietnam to combat malnutrition.  However, when Sternin arrived, he was given \\"six months to make a difference\\".  This deadline made the research Sternin collected on malnutrition root causes -- poverty, lack of clean water, poor sanitation and nutrition ignorance -- \\"true but useless\\" (TBU).<br /><br />Sternin didn't have the time or money to address the underlying issues.  He needed to identify a more direct way to make a difference.  Forgoing the typical \\"focus on the problem\\" route, Sternin set out in search of bright spots.  Sternin sought out well-nourished village children to learn how they defied the odds.  Once identified, Sternin studied how these homes varied from the norm, on the lookout for deviations related to nutrition.  Through observation, Sternin was able to identify differences in what and how the well-nourished children were fed.<br /><br />Instead of issuing a proclamation of his findings,  Sternin created a change path.  Sternin instituted a mothers-teaching-mothers community program to change feeding habits.  Making these seemingly small adjustments - number of meals per day, individual servings, sweet potato green and shrimp supplements to rice -- dramatically improved childhood nutrition in the studied villages, and then spread throughout the country, eventually reaching 2.2 million people in 265 villages.<br /><br />Besides the humanitarian aspect, what appealed to me about Sternin's story was the problem solving technique.  Instead of getting lost in \\"true but useless\\" (analysis-paralysis) Sternin identified and exploited bright spots.  In a later example, a similar technique is described as Appreciative Inquiry.<br /><br />Reading Switch, I picked up techniques related to change and solving hard problems.  The latter was a pleasant surprise for me.  If your work involves change, hard problems or the combination, I highly recommend Switch.

(There are upwards of thirty or forty \\"Discworld\\" books total, which cluster into subgroups around individual characters). While you could certainly start here if you wanted to, new readers might find it more rewarding to begin with [[ASIN:0060012382 The Wee Free Men]], the first in Tiffany's sub-series, followed by [[ASIN:0060586621 A Hat Full of Sky]] and then [[ASIN:0060890339 Wintersmith]], before proceeding to this one.<br /><br />This is billed as a children's / young adult book, although little sets it apart from Pratchett's other fantasy except for some (very) slight bowdlerizations; primarily, this is a young adult book because the heroine is a young adult, and it deals with issues that young adults have to deal with.  Like the Harry Potter books, the content and tone of the Tiffany series have been maturing ever so slightly with each book to match the advancing maturity of the protagonist, and while this one's still suitable for younger readers, it definitely contains a few jokes likely to fly over their heads (at least unless some other source has educated them).  Tiffany herself is portrayed as very mature for her age - a portrayal deliberate on Pratchett's part, I believe, as Tiffany is exactly as mature as most people that age tend to think they are, and almost as mature as she herself wants to be.<br /><br />Each volume in the series sets Tiffany a particular problem to resolve; here the problem (s) are innuendo, rumour, gossip, romantic rivalries, and pointless mob hatred, things that many if not most teenage girls will identify with (even if in Tiffany's case the \\"witch hunt\\" she has to deal with is somewhat more literal). 0765350386|5.0|Let me start by saying that I have read all three books in this trilogy multiple times, and I love all of them. Brandon Sanderson is one of my favorite authors and, in my opinion, one of the best in the genre today. Now, the review:    This is the story of, in Mr. Sanderson's own words, what would have happened had the evil lord won. And yet, it's so much more than that. It's a heist story, about a thieving crew who attempts to steal an empire. It's a coming-of-age tale, about a teenage thief who's lived a hard life. It's a story about trust, and friendship, and, in the end, why we keep fighting even when there's no chance of winning. The author takes some of the best ideas in fantasy and mixes them together, throwing in a brilliant magic system and memorable characters in as well.    The magic system is indeed marvelous. The idea of Allomancy is that you use metals to give yourself different boosts, or to affect the emotions of others, or to move metal. It's so well integrated and well explained in the book that I never really feel confused about what's going on. In addition, Sanderson hints at another two systems of magic: Feruchemy, which is the system Keepers can use, and another that Inquisitors use. Simply put, I feel like his magic is real and comprehensible, which is something you don't see in every fantasy novel.    Next, the characters. Vin is a thief girl who at the beginning of the book at least, distrusts everyone and has no friends. Watching her grow to trust the members of her crew is a joy. Kelsier as well is fascinating, with his shadowed past and his humor. He is one of the best characters in the book. Elend, the only character in the book who is a full noble, makes me laugh every time I read about him. He is quite possibly my favorite of all the characters, and that's saying something as I love all of them. Then we have the backup members of the crew: Breeze, Ham, Dox, Sazed, Spook, Clubs, and Marsh, who add levity to what would otherwise be a dark tale and provide different perspectives and wisdom throughout the book.    Last, I am always astounded by the depth and knowledge of religion Sanderson puts into all his books. He doesn't pass over the issues, showing what can happen when a religious figure becomes a tyrant. But he also shows the power and beauty religion can have, and the religious diversity that exists in his world.    Sanderson is my favorite author, and this book, and indeed the trilogy, are his best works. The beginning engrossed me, the middle made me laugh, and I cried at parts of the end. It takes a truly talented author to do that.

B003A4IFGY|4.0|This is Mike Campbell's baby.  Campbell, if you didn't know, is the seriously underrated shred-capable lead guitarist for the Heartbreakers. Like you've never heard before, he kills with his divine '59 Gibson Les Paul, maybe the most desired guitar in existence.  Please, appreciate: each ripple of reverb, the tonal entirety of this six-string dream's palette.    With Tom Petty finally giving his band its due, the liner notes detail the make and vintage of each instrument, each track's made-on date.  Mojo tries to stick as close to live takes as possible, forgoing excessive dubbing, capturing same-room interplay.  This is their music-for-musicians album; this is their Ur-rock blues album.       Mojo opens with \\"Jefferson Jericho Blues\\"--interesting subject matter, that prez's lustful miscegeny--but it's hard to get past the constant harmonica blasts.  \\"Jefferson\\" is a little goofy, especially to open the album.  They'd have been better off with the second cut, \\"First Flash of Freedom,\\" which melodically sounds like a companion piece to \\"The Golden Rose,\\" the superb finale off Petty's last disc, Highway Companion--but more Hammond Organ flourishes and erupting axe workouts.  It's Campbell that elevates \\"First Flash\\" with his liquid slide-guitar lope, reminiscent here of Pink Floyd Stratocaster master David Gilmour's proggy '70s perfection.  The lyrics, as elsewhere on the album, are the Petty basics--old towns with no name, overdue trains--but the song is suggestive enough.  Does its surrealism amount to nascent drug use, sexual awakening, or something else altogether?      But then with \\"Running Man's Bible\\" we get genre exercise, a six minute chug, with vocals patterned off the old call-and-response verses that have been around forever; likewise with \\"Let Yourself Go,\\" where there's the same pitch drop at the end of the lines.  Enjoyable, but not memorable.  Yet another cousin to this style, \\"Candy\\" is bloodless, bringing nothing new to its 12-bar blues.  There'sno doubt Petty is enjoying himself with some in-jokes, but he's outrun his voice, which was never full-throated to begin with.  A few of Mojo's roots calisthenics come up hokey and half-baked.        \\"No Reason to Cry\\" is a middling pedal steel lullaby, inoffensive as far as this kind of song goes.  Conversely, the album swerves violent on the next number.  \\"I Should Have Known It\\" is hard-shelled, as dirty as the Heartbreakers get, with drums descended from John Bonham and a riff off Jimmy Page's Les Paul reborn--Listen and compare with Zep's \\"The Ocean\\".  Petty's unique inflection/phrasing interacts with this new setting, coming out snarling and defiant.  Still, in spots he sounds tired, for all the blistering fretwork backing him.      Nevertheless, Mojo ambitiously maps out a small universe of the blues and its manifestations.  \\"US 41,\\" where Petty does something distinctly Appalachian with his voice, is almost Johnny Cash-like.  Since every self-respecting bluesman has to have a devil piece, there's \\"High in the Morning,\\" complete with those blasphemous backwards guitar lines.  The cathartic closer \\"Good Enough\\" follows in the line of Zeppelin's \\"Since I've Been Lovin' You\\" and Floyd's \\"Shine On You Crazy Diamond\\" brand of apocalypse blues, all musical damnation and wailing, doomed guitar.  An instant classic.         \\"Lover's Touch\\" brings to mind the Heartbreaker's hit \\"Breakdown,\\" with a heavier glaze.  This is where we can see the sense in undertaking Mojo--this stuff is the bedrock of their music.  The dreamy \\"Trip to Pirate's Cove\\" is one of Petty's hypnotic down-tempo tunes a la \\"Night Driver\\" and \\"Face in the Crowd,\\" et al.  And then, well, there's reggae... Yes, the herbal \\"Don't Pull Me Over\\" is, I believe, Petty's first Jamaican-style jam, and not bad if you can get past that.      Penultimately (before the closing statement of \\"Good Enough\\"), Petty serves us a down-to-earth song that echoes \\"Learning to Fly.\\"  Displaying his most natural gift, \\"Something Good Coming\\" is the hopeful fable of which he is always capable, no matter the year of release.  Is TP rock's Hans Christen Anderson?  He seems to create his contemporary fairy tales effortlessly; always familiar and archetypal, always equal parts simple and good.      Mojo is the guitar album the heretofore jangle-pop Heartbreakers have never made.  You're sure to hear Allman Brothers comparisons, valid since both bands share fiery guitar duels sweating out North Florida swamp water.  And certainly, Petty's 2008 resurrection of his first band Mudcrutch had a hand in this.  There may never be another hit-laden Full Moon Fever, but ask yourself, how often do we get to watch a mature band get better?  This just doesn't happen, and it's our pleasure.  It's steroidal Barry Bonds and Roger Clemens turning back the clock--but embrace this one, because there are no performance-enhancing drugs for musicians.  Well, there are, and we celebrate that.  But that's not what's at play here; it's something deeper and more rewarding.  This is their mojo we're talking about.

B003AO1SVS|5.0|The Black Keys gone glam?  \\"Everlasting Light,\\" catchy in its clap-stomp bounce from the get-go, is pure T-Rex redux.  Its falsetto vocals and perfectly dumb couplets (\\"Sun/when there is none,\\") are set off by sexy shushes.  This does not mark a one-tune detour.  On glammed-up \\"Howlin' for You,\\" trebly fuzz guitar needles through a sliding tempo tribute to Gary Glitter and bleachers-quaking rock.  The percussion (Carney's kit drives both the song and album) overpowers anything besides the mindless sing-along chorus, but apparently there's some baseball innuendo in there.       Brothers seems to look forward by looking back.  A Motown/soul revival shows on \\"Unknown Brother,\\" the Jerry Butler cover \\"Never Gonna Give You Up,\\" and the closer \\"These Days.\\"  Period piece gems, each, with strikingly un-Keysian touches: delicate glockenspiel on \\"Days\\"; sleigh bells on \\"Brother\\" that jingle under the radar until the other instruments drop out for a moment of what Weezy would call \\"letting the beat build.\\"       And at that hip reference, I'm reminded of the vertiginous new-found breadth of this supposed raw blues duo--there is even hip hop here.  \\"Too Afraid to Love You,\\" built around a steely, Wu-Tangish harpsichord loop, reveals the influence of Wu producer extraordinaire RZA, with whom Auerbach teamed on the rap-blues experiment album Balckroc between 2008's Attack & Release and Brothers.  The miracle is that \\"Too Afraid\\" sits just fine--maybe even unnoticeably--within this record's 15-track sequencing.    Of course, you have your good old dirty electric blues, too.  The Keys didn't throw out their signature sound with all this aural manifest destiny.  They've got all their swagger on \\"Next Girl,\\" who will be nothing like their \\"ex-girl\\", and Auerbach makes sure to unspool a crunchy guitar lick as the song expires. (Side note, check out this one's awesome viral video--a Playboy grotto's worth of girls poolside and a lip-syncing dinosaur hand puppet.  Just imagine.)  The strong-melodied \\"Ten Cent Pistol\\" sounds like Screamin' Jay Hawkins on \\"I Put a Spell on You,\\"--spooked-out 50s blues.     \\"Black Mud\\" is just that--Zeppelin sludge, organic, thick, stirred by blues guitar as an organ holds sance.  The album's only instrumental, it allows the listener to focus on its chunky texture and swirl of tone colors; offbeat, it's a subtle fast-favorite.  The following track \\"The Only One\\" takes that same sonic mud and shapes it into a pretty-chorused earthwork chapel, but it could probably benefit from truncation--it's basically the same musical phrase for five minutes.  Conversely, though, that repetition is hypnotic--you wouldn't suggest editing down the Beatles' \\"I Want You (She's So Heavy),\\" would you?      And then you've got \\"She's Long Gone,\\" which I can only describe as great filler.  Good rhythm, decent verses, anonymous chorus, overlaying a solid riff: just the (great) boilerplate blues we're used to.  But where Brothers really shines is the few spots they nail the blend of old and new.  \\"Sinister Kid\\" is a Beck-like amalgamation, catchy, weird and smart.  It's a beat that could be rapped over, kicked off with a James Brown grunt; danceable but still musically legit.  There's even a country-tinged solo to put the lid on this mostly funked-up cut.  The other high note is the lead single, \\"Tighten Up,\\" a mix of Sixties pop and Keys blues (guest-producer Danger Mouse).  You're hooked from the first damn whistle of the intro.  Oriental-flavored guitar interludes, customary irresistible clichd lyrics, and a burbling breakdown tempo shift to the second half will fill your heart with amplifier distortion.          You couldn't ask much more of a white blues guitar-drum duo in 2010--maybe less, as the album runs fairly long.    There's nothing here to rival the pure rawness of 2004's Rubber Factory, but credit the Black Keys for not rehashing their successful formula and instead serving up this alchemy of remarkably fresh retro styles.And they're still your go-to for re-creating the genesis of dirty rock.  As the Boston Globe said of Danger Mouse-produced Attack & Release, \\"Cleaning up the boys won't stop them from tracking mud all over the house.\\"
